The Dream of Ahmad ibn Yusuf al-Isbahani: A Prophetic Endorsement
A dream narrative endorsing Ibn al-Anbari and al-Marwazi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dream narrative is used by Ibn Asakir to validate the scholarly credentials of Abu Bakr ibn al-Anbari and Abu Ishaq al-Marwazi.

Core Idea
Dreams can corroborate scholarly authority, but only when supported by reliable transmission.

Ibn Asakir presents a detailed chain of transmission for a dream in which the Prophet Muhammad directs a seeker to take Quran from Ibn al-Anbari and fiqh from al-Marwazi. This dream serves to highlight the stature of these scholars, particularly al-Marwazi, who was a teacher of al-Ash'ari. The passage shows how traditionalist scholars used such narratives to bolster the credibility of key figures.

Guided Path
1
Read the dream narrative
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the chain of transmission.
2
Identify the two scholars
Note who the Prophet recommends for Quran and for fiqh, and consider why these names matter.
3
Understand the chain
The chain is long; note that Ibn Asakir is a hadith scholar, so he provides a full sanad.
4
Connect to kalam
Recall that Abu Ishaq al-Marwazi was a Shafi'i scholar; his student al-Ash'ari studied under him. This dream indirectly supports kalam by honoring a kalam-affiliated scholar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أخبرنا الشريف أبو القسم على بن إبراهيم بن العباس الحسينى وأبو الحسن على ابن أحمد بن منصور الغساني قال ثنا وأبو منصور عبد الرحمن بن محمد الشيباني قال أنا أبو بكر أحمد بن على ابن ثابت الحافظ قال أخبرنى على ابن المحسن القاضي قال ثنا أبو إسحق إبراهيم ابن أحمد المعدل قال سمعت أبا جعفر محمد بن محمد بن أحمد بن عبد الله المقرى يقول قال لي أحمد بن محمد بن يوسف الأصبهاني وهو ابن أختى رأيت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في النوم فقلت يا رسول الله عمن آخذ القرآن فقال عن أبي بكر بن الأنباري فقلت فالفقه قال عن أبي إسحق المروزي. ولئن صحح حكاية البربهاري وقال بثبوتها فلقد نعته وطائفته بالجهل وهو أخص نعوتها هل يرد على اليهود والنصارى أو المجوس بقول أحمد إلا ذو اللب المعكوس وإن زعم أن مجادلة أهل الكتاب لا تجوز.
And al-Sharif Abu al-Qasim 'Ali ibn Ibrahim ibn al-'Abbas al-Husayni and Abu al-Hasan 'Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Mansur al-Ghassani informed us, saying: ... and Abu Mansur 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Muhammad al-Shaybani said: ... Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn 'Ali ibn Thabit al-Hafiz told us: ... 'Ali ibn al-Muhsin al-Qadi told me: ... Abu Ishaq Ibrahim ibn Ahmad al-Mu'addil told us: ... I heard Abu Ja'far Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn 'Abd Allah al-Muqri' saying: Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Yusuf al-Isbahani, who is my sister's son, said to me: I saw the Prophet (peace be upon him) in a dream and said, 'O Messenger of Allah, from whom should I take the Quran?' He said, 'From Abu Bakr ibn al-Anbari.' I said, 'And fiqh?' He said, 'From Abu Ishaq al-Marwazi.' And if he [i.e., the narrator of the Barbahari story] authenticates the story of al-Barbahari and asserts its establishment, then he has indeed described him and his sect with ignorance, and that is the most specific of their descriptions. Can one refute the Jews and Christians or the Magians with the statement of Ahmad except one of inverted intellect? And if he claims that debating the People of the Book is not permissible [then he is mistaken].
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage contains two parts: first, a chain of transmission leading to a dream in which the Prophet Muhammad instructs a person to take the Quran from Abu Bakr ibn al-Anbari and fiqh from Abu Ishaq al-Marwazi. Ibn Asakir uses this dream to establish the high status of these scholars, particularly Abu Ishaq al-Marwazi, who was a leading Shafi'i scholar. Second, Ibn Asakir directly refutes the story of al-Barbahari. He argues that if anyone claims the story is authentic, they have described al-Barbahari and his followers as ignorant—which is indeed their most characteristic trait. Then Ibn Asakir poses a rhetorical question: How can one refute Jews, Christians, or Magians using only the statements of Ahmad ibn Hanbal, unless one has an inverted intellect? This is a defense of kalam as necessary for debating non-Muslims. He also dismisses the notion that debating the People of the Book is forbidden, implying that those who reject kalam cannot properly defend Islam.

Key Terms
أبو بكر بن الأنباري
أبو إسحق المروزي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the dream, from whom did the Prophet instruct the seeker to take fiqh?
A) Abu Bakr ibn al-Anbari
B) Abu Ishaq al-Marwazi
C) Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari
D) Ahmad ibn Hanbal

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir uses a Prophetic dream to affirm the scholarly authority of Abu Ishaq al-Marwazi, linking him to the Ash'ari tradition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Prophet appeared to you in a dream and asked you from whom you learned your religion, would you be able to name reliable scholars with known chains? What does your answer reveal about your responsibility in seeking knowledge properly?
?
This dream highlights the importance of learning from qualified teachers. Who are the teachers you have learned your Islam from? Do you know their chain of knowledge back to the Prophet? How can you verify that what you have learned is sound?
?
Ibn Asakir transmitted this dream with a meticulous chain. How carefully do you check the sources of religious information you encounter? Are there any teachings you accepted without verifying their authenticity? What concrete step will you take this week to ensure your knowledge comes from trustworthy sources?

Ibn Asakir's Defense of Kalam: Refuting the Barbahari Story
A refutation of the Barbahari story and defense of kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Asakir's argument that rejecting kalam makes it impossible to refute Jews and Christians, and that the Barbahari story is false.

Core Idea
Rejecting kalam leaves Muslims defenseless against intellectual attacks from other religions.

Ibn Asakir directly challenges the story of al-Barbahari, which was used to discredit kalam. He argues that authenticating that story would only show al-Barbahari's ignorance, and then poses a rhetorical question: how can one debate Jews and Christians using only the statements of Ahmad ibn Hanbal, unless one has an inverted intellect? This powerfully defends the necessity of kalam for interfaith dialogue.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic from 'ولئن صحح' to the end. Note the rhetorical force.
2
Identify the target
Ibn Asakir is refuting the Barbahari story. Who was al-Barbahari? A Hanbali figure known for opposing kalam.
3
Analyze the argument
He says if the story is true, it shows Barbahari's ignorance. Then he asks about debating non-Muslims. What is his point?
4
Connect to kalam's necessity
Understand that kalam provides rational arguments needed to refute other religions.

Refuting the Claim that Debating Ahl al-Kitab is Forbidden
Refutation of al-Barbahari's prohibition of debating Ahl al-Kitab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ibn 'Asakir refutes al-Barbahari's claim that debating Ahl al-Kitab is forbidden, using Quranic evidence and the method of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari.

Core Idea
Debating Ahl al-Kitab is commanded by the Quran, not forbidden.

Al-Barbahari and his followers claimed that debating Jews and Christians is impermissible, contradicting the clear Quranic command. This passage confronts that claim head-on, showing that such a position stems from ignorance and a lack of proper understanding of the proofs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recognize that al-Barbahari and his faction oppose dialectical debate with Ahl al-Kitab. The author begins by pointing out that confirming the anecdote (the dream story) only highlights their ignorance.
The anecdote is likely the dream of Ahmad ibn Hanbal being questioned by the Prophet.
2
Analyze the Quranic proof
The author directly quotes Quran 29:46, which commands debating Ahl al-Kitab in the best way. This is a clear refutation of the claim that debate is forbidden.
3
Recognize the significance of Abu al-Hasan's method
Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) is presented as the one who provided proofs and explained the path. The author argues that those who rely on fabrications attributed to Ahmad cannot clarify proofs.
This reinforces the Ash'ari methodology of dialectical theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولئن صحح حكاية البربهاري (1) وقال بثبوتها فلقد نعته وطائفته بالجهل وهو أخص نعوتها هل يرد على اليهود والنصارى أو المجوس بقول أحمد إلا ذو اللب المعكوس وإن زعم أن مجادلة أهل الكتاب لا تجوز ولا تستحسن فقد قال الله تعالى : ولا تجادلوا أهل الكتاب إلا بالتي هي أحسن وهو ما ذكره أبو الحسن من الحجج وشرحه ربه لمن أراد سلوك طريقه فيه وأوضحه ولو احتج محتج على مخالفي الملة ... موصات أحمد بن حنبل لم يصح له إيضاح الادلة
And if he (al-Barbahari) authenticated the narration and said it is established, then he and his faction have been described with ignorance, which is the most specific of their descriptions. Does anyone respond to the Jews, Christians, or Magians with the statement of Ahmad except one of inverted intellect? And if he claims that debating the People of the Book is not permissible nor commendable, God Almighty has said: 'And do not argue with the People of the Book except in the best way' – and that is what Abu al-Hasan mentioned of proofs, and his Lord explained it for whoever wishes to follow his path therein, and made it clear. And if someone were to argue against those who oppose the religion ... the fabrications of Ahmad ibn Hanbal, he would not have valid clarification of the proofs.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Barbahari's position that debating the People of the Book (Jews and Christians) is forbidden. The author begins by noting that if al-Barbahari confirms the report (the dream anecdote) and asserts its authenticity, he and his group have thereby been characterized by ignorance – that being their most distinctive trait. The author then poses a rhetorical question: only a person of inverted intellect would respond to Jews, Christians, or Magians with the statement of Ahmad (ibn Hanbal) [i.e., rely solely on Ahmad's authority without proof]. Then the author addresses al-Barbahari's specific claim that debating Ahl al-Kitab is not permissible or commendable. He refutes it by quoting the Qur'anic verse (29:46): 'And do not argue with the People of the Book except in the best way.' The author then asserts that this is exactly what Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) did – he provided proofs and arguments, and God explained and clarified the path for those who wish to follow it. Finally, the author says that if someone were to argue against those who oppose the religion by relying on fabricated reports attributed to Ahmad ibn Hanbal, that person would not have valid clarification of the proofs. Thus, the author defends Ash'ari's method of dialectical theology against the traditionalist rejection of debate.

Key Terms
مجادلة أهل الكتاب
البربهاري
أبو الحسن
قول أحمد
الجهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ibn Hanbal absolutely forbade all debate with non-Muslims.
Correction: The Quran commands debating Ahl al-Kitab in the best way, and Ahmad ibn Hanbal's own statements do not override the Quran; fabrications attributed to him are not valid proofs.

Checkpoint
What Qur'anic verse does the author cite to refute the claim that debating Ahl al-Kitab is forbidden?
A) 'Invite to the way of your Lord with wisdom'
B) 'And do not argue with the People of the Book except in the best way'
C) 'Say: O People of the Book, come to a common word'
D) 'Fight those who do not believe in God'

Key Takeaway
The Quran explicitly permits and encourages debating Ahl al-Kitab in the best manner, and this is the method of the Ash'ari school.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever avoided discussing Islam with a non-Muslim because you felt it was rude or forbidden? How does this Quranic command change your duty?
?
When you engage in discussions about religion, do you ensure you are arguing 'in the best way'? What one habit do you need to change to obey this verse?
?
Are you willing to challenge a respected authority (like al-Barbahari) when their view contradicts the Quran? What fear holds you back from doing so?

Refutation of the Slander That al-Ash'ari Had No Religion
Refutation of the main slander and critique of anthropomorphists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the accusation that al-Ash'ari had no religion, and see how he criticizes anthropomorphists and modern reformers who spread such slanders.

Core Idea
The slander against al-Ash'ari's religion is baseless, and the author exposes the flawed reasoning of its proponents.

In this passage, the author tackles one of the most severe slanders against Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari: that he was irreligious. He not only denies the charge but also diagnoses the roots of such falsehoods in anthropomorphic tendencies and misguided reform movements. Let's see how he builds his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the slander
Begin by reading the opening lines where the author presents the accusation against al-Ash'ari. Notice the strong language used by the accuser and how the author frames his response.
The author immediately calls the accuser's faction 'vile'.
2
Explore the critique of anthropomorphism
The author uses the accusation as a springboard to criticize the anthropomorphist tendencies of al-Barbahari, Ibn al-Qayyim, and the Hashwiyya. Pay attention to how he links their flawed interpretations to the slander against al-Ash'ari.
This shows the author's broader concern with theological correctness.
3
Analyze the final refutation
Returning to the main point, the author denies the accusation and questions its credibility. Note how he appeals to the consensus of scholars and rational people, and dismisses the opponents as biased.
The refutation is concise but powerful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ( أنه قد ثبت وصح بنقل الفضلاء أنه كان لادين له ومما يذيب قلوب الغيورين على هذا الدين الحنيف دين الفطرة أن يروا دوام هذه النحلة الرديئة مدى الدهر ، وليس بغريب من مثل البربهاري فى بعده عن العلم هذه البدعة وإنما الغريب أن يذكر مثل ابن القيم فى كتابه ( بدائع الفرائد ( في صفحة ٣٩ من الجزء الرابع منه أن المراد بالمقام المحمود أقعاد الرسول على العرش وأن يسرد جماعة من الحشوية - خلا من لم يثبت ذلك عنهم - ذهبوا إلى ذلك بطريقة قد تنطلي على الضعفاء في العلم ، والأغرب من هذا وذاك أن يرفع دعاة الاصلاح العصرى عقيرتهم بالدعوة إلى تقلد مذهب من يكون بهذه السخافة في بداهة العقول والإعلان عنه وعن شيخه ( الذي يقول فيما رد به على أساس التقديس للرازى عند الكلام في الاستواء : ولو شاء لاستقر على ظهر بعوضة فاستقلت به بقدرته فكيف على عرش عظيم . ( أنهما أكبر مصلح تتطلبه حاجة العصر فإن كان هذا هو الاصلاح فعلى الإسلام السلام وليستغرق شيوخ السنة في سباتهم العميق والتغاضى عما يتطور من البدع حتى ينقذ القضاء لئلا يروا ما يسجل لهم التاريخ . وإلى الله المشتكى وإن إلى ربك الرجعي . فغير صحيح من صح ذلك عند أمثاله عند العلماء والعقلاء فعند من صح السالمية أم صدق فيه أقوال أعدائه من المعتزلة والجهمية ؟ فإن أراد أنه قد عنده فإنه بحمد الله لا عند له وكيف يصدق مثله عليه ، وقد تبينت سوء اعتقاده وخطله. وأما حكايته عن أبي الحسن الشاهد بالأهواز فعن مجهول لم يعرف إلا بالسقط والاحتراز ومقالته خارجة عن حد الاهتدال تنبئ عنه إنه كان من القائلين بالاعتزال لأنه جعل الخروج عن مذهب أهل الاعتزال الحادا وكفى بهذا القدر من قوله فسادا . فأما تشبيهه أبا الحسن بابن الروندى فإنه فيه غير مصيب عندى فقد ذكرت تسمية ما نقض عليه أبو الحسن من تواليفه وبين من فساد أقواله فى كتبه وتصانيفه فكيف يقرن بينهما في الالحاد مع ما كان بينهما من الخلاف والعناد . وأما حكايته عن أخيه أحمد بن على الأهوازي في بويلة العيد وأنه لم يصل عشرين سنة فمن الكذب المستنكر البعيد (۱) فمن يعرف بالعدالة أخاه ومن ذا يصدقه
And as for his statement that it has been established and confirmed through transmission by virtuous men that he had no religion - and among what melts the hearts of those who are zealous for this upright religion, the religion of natural disposition, is that they see the persistence of this vile faction forever. And it is not surprising from someone like al-Barbahari, in his distance from knowledge, this innovation. But rather the surprising thing is that someone like Ibn al-Qayyim mentions in his book 'Bada'i' al-Fara'id' on page 39 of the fourth part that what is meant by the Praiseworthy Station is seating the Messenger on the Throne, and that he lists a group of the Hashwiyya - except for those for whom it is not confirmed - who went to that in a way that may deceive the weak in knowledge. And more surprising than this and that is that the modern reform preachers raise their voices calling for adopting the school of one who has such foolishness in the basic axioms of intellects, and announcing about him and about his teacher - who says in his refutation of 'Asas al-Taqdis' of al-Razi when discussing al-istiwa': 'If He willed, He would settle on the back of a mosquito and it would carry Him by His power, so how much more on a mighty Throne.' - that they are the greatest reformer that the need of the era requires. If this is reform, then peace be on Islam, and let the Sunni scholars sink into their deep sleep and disregard the development of innovations until the decree saves them lest they see what history will record against them. And to Allah is the complaint, and surely to your Lord is the return. Then it is not correct. Who considers that correct among his likes among scholars and rational men? According to whom is it correct? Is it confirmed that [al-Ash'ari] was without religion? Or were the statements of his enemies among the Mu'tazila and Jahmiyya believed about him? If he means that he has that [accusation] with him, then by Allah's praise, he does not have [such] with him. And how can such a thing be believed about him, and his evil belief and error have become clear? And as for his story about Abu al-Hasan the witness from Ahwaz, it is from an unknown person, known only for being discarded and caution, and his statement goes beyond the bounds of moderation, indicating that he was among those who say al-i'tizal, because he considered leaving the school of the Mu'tazila to be heresy. This amount of his statement is sufficient as corruption. As for his comparing Abu al-Hasan to Ibn al-Rawandi, then he is not correct in my view, for I have mentioned the naming of what Abu al-Hasan refuted from his writings and clarified the corruption of his statements in his books and works. So how can he equate them in heresy, given the opposition and hostility between them? And as for his story about his brother Ahmad ibn Ali al-Ahwazi concerning the urine of the festival [or: the 'Buwayla' of the Eid] and that he did not pray for twenty years, it is from the clearly denied and far-fetched lies. So who knows his brother to be just? And who will believe him?
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive refutation of multiple slanders against Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. The author begins by quoting the accusation that al-Ash'ari had no religion. He then launches into a lengthy critique of the anthropomorphist faction (which he considers a vile faction), criticizing al-Barbahari and Ibn al-Qayyim for spreading false interpretations about the Praiseworthy Station. He also attacks modern reformers who advocate for a school that holds crude anthropomorphic views. Returning to the specific refutation, he asserts that the accusation is false and asks who among scholars and rational people would accept it. He notes that al-Ash'ari's enemies (Mu'tazila and Jahmiyya) are not reliable sources. Then he addresses three more specific allegations: first, about a certain witness in Ahwaz who is an unknown and unreliable person; second, the comparison of al-Ash'ari to the heretic Ibn al-Rawandi, which the author refutes by pointing out that al-Ash'ari himself wrote refutations of Ibn al-Rawandi; third, a story about his brother not praying for twenty years, which he dismisses as a far-fetched lie. The author uses strong language to defend al-Ash'ari's honor.

البربهاري
ابن القيم
المقام المحمود
الحشوية
الاصلاح العصرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari had no religion and was an irreligious person.
Correction: The author refutes this by stating it is not correct, that the accusers are biased enemies, and that no scholar or rational person would accept it.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the accusation that al-Ash'ari had no religion?
A) He says it is a lie spread by his enemies like the Mu'tazila and Jahmiyya.
B) He provides a philosophical proof of al-Ash'ari's religiosity.
C) He ignores the accusation and focuses on other issues.
D) He admits the accusation but argues it does not matter.

Key Takeaway
The author vehemently denies the slander that al-Ash'ari was irreligious, attributing it to enemies and exposing the flawed reasoning of those who spread it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a rumor about a scholar, do you immediately assume it's true or do you investigate the source? How can you train yourself to verify before believing? Will you commit to always seeking evidence from reliable sources before forming an opinion about anyone's faith?
?
This passage shows that even great scholars like Ibn al-Qayyim can fall into error. How does that challenge your trust in blindly following any scholar? Which scholars do you follow, and have you ever examined their beliefs critically? What will you do to ensure you are not misled by seemingly learned people?
?
The author is deeply grieved by the persistence of false accusations against al-Ash'ari. Do you feel any similar grief when you see the reputation of a righteous person being attacked? How can you defend the honor of scholars and believers when you hear slander? Will you speak up or remain silent?

Rebuttal of Specific Slanders Against al-Ash'ari
Refutation of three specific slanders about a witness, Ibn al-Rawandi, and a brother.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author rebuts three specific slanders against al-Ash'ari: the Ahwazi witness, the comparison to Ibn al-Rawandi, and the story about his brother.

Core Idea
The author dismantles each slander by exposing its weak source, false comparison, and blatant fabrication.

After refuting the main accusation, the author addresses three more specific attacks on al-Ash'ari's character. Each is shown to be based on unreliable informants or malicious distortions. Let's examine how he defends his honor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three slanders
The author lists three separate allegations: a) a witness from Ahwaz claimed al-Ash'ari was irreligious, b) comparing al-Ash'ari to the heretic Ibn al-Rawandi, c) a story about his brother not praying for twenty years.
These are typical slanders spread by opponents.
2
Examine the rebuttal of the witness
The author dismisses the witness as an unknown and unreliable person, and points out that his statement indicates he was a Mu'tazili who considered leaving their school as heresy.
This discredits the source.
3
Analyze the comparison to Ibn al-Rawandi
The author refutes this by noting that al-Ash'ari himself wrote refutations of Ibn al-Rawandi's works, showing their intellectual opposition.
They cannot be equated.
4
Consider the brother's story
The author calls this a far-fetched lie, asking who would know the brother to be just and who would believe such a tale.
This highlights the lack of evidence.

Defending al-Ash'ari from Heresy
Ibn Asakir refutes the comparison of al-Ash'ari to Ibn al-Rawandi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir systematically refutes the accusation that al-Ash'ari was a heretic like Ibn al-Rawandi.

Core Idea
al-Ash'ari refuted Ibn al-Rawandi; they are opposites, not allies.

This passage shows Ibn Asakir defending al-Ash'ari against slander. He dismantles the accusation by first questioning its credibility, then exposing the unreliability of its source, and finally pointing out that al-Ash'ari actually authored refutations of Ibn al-Rawandi. Follow along as he builds his case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the rhetorical questions and the direct refutation.
2
Identify the three refutations
Ibn Asakir makes three points: the claim is baseless, the source is an unknown Mu'tazili, and al-Ash'ari refuted Ibn al-Rawandi. Find each.
The third point is the strongest.
3
Connect to the learning objective
Consider how each point undermines the accusation. Why is the third point decisive?
Because it shows direct opposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فغير صحيح. من صح ذلك عنده أمثاله عند العلماء والعقلاء فعند من صح السالمية أم صدق فيه أقوال أعدائه من المعتزلة والجهمية؟ فإن أراد أنه قد عنده فإنه بحمد الله لا عند له وكيف يصدق مثله عليه، وقد تبينت سوء اعتقاده وخطله. وأما حكايته عن أبي الحسن الشاهد بالأهواز فعن مجهول لم يعرف إلا بالسقط والاحتراز ومقالته خارجة عن حد الاهتدال تنبئ عنه إنه كان من القائلين بالاعتزال لأنه جعل الخروج عن مذهب أهل الاعتزال الحادا وكفى بهذا القدر من قوله فسادا. فأما تشبيهه أبا الحسن بابن الروندى فإنه فيه غير مصيب عندى فقد ذكرت تسمية ما نقض عليه أبو الحسن من تواليفه وبين من فساد أقواله فى كتبه وتصانيفه فكيف يقرن بينهما في الالحاد مع ما كان بينهما من الخلاف والعناد.
It is not correct. Who would believe that among his peers among scholars and intelligent people? Or would he believe the words of his enemies from the Mu'tazila and Jahmiyya? If he means that he has something, then by God he has nothing of it. How could one believe such a thing about him, when his evil belief and error have become apparent? As for his story about Abu al-Hasan al-Shahid al-Ahwazi, it is from an unknown person not known except for fall and precaution, and his statement is beyond the limit of moderation, indicating that he was one of the proponents of i'tizal because he considered leaving the madhhab of the people of i'tizal to be heresy. This amount of his statement is sufficient to show its corruption. As for his likening Abu al-Hasan to Ibn al-Rawandi, he is not correct in my view, for I have mentioned the naming of the works that Abu al-Hasan refuted against him and demonstrated the corruption of his statements in his books and writings. So how can he equate them in heresy, given the disagreement and hostility between them?
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ahwazi's accusations. Ibn Asakir begins by stating that the claim is false. He uses a rhetorical question: who among scholars and intelligent people would accept such a claim, especially when it comes from the enemies of al-Ash'ari, the Mu'tazila and Jahmiyya? He then addresses the specific claim that Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) was influenced by a certain 'Abu al-Hasan al-Shahid al-Ahwazi', showing that this person is unknown and his statement indicates he was a Mu'tazili. Finally, he refutes the comparison of al-Ash'ari to the notorious heretic Ibn al-Rawandi, noting that al-Ash'ari actually wrote refutations of Ibn al-Rawandi's works, so they cannot be equated.

Key Terms
ابن الروندي
الاعتزال
الملحد
أبو الحسن الأشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Ash'ari had heretical leanings similar to Ibn al-Rawandi.
Correction: Ibn Asakir shows that al-Ash'ari actually refuted Ibn al-Rawandi's works, demonstrating their complete opposition.

Checkpoint
How does Ibn Asakir refute al-Ahwazi's claim that al-Ash'ari was like Ibn al-Rawandi?
A) By quoting the Qur'an
B) By stating that al-Ash'ari refuted Ibn al-Rawandi's works
C) By saying al-Ash'ari was ignorant
D) By denying the existence of Ibn al-Rawandi

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir refutes the comparison by revealing the unreliability of the accuser and demonstrating al-Ash'ari's direct opposition to Ibn al-Rawandi's heresy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been accused of something false based on your associations? How did you feel, and how can Ibn Asakir's method—tracing the accusation to its biased source—help you respond with truth rather than emotion?
?
When you hear a negative claim about a scholar of Islam, do you immediately accept it or do you investigate the accuser's credibility? What would it take for you to be as thorough as Ibn Asakir before forming an opinion?
?
Think of a person you have unfairly compared to someone else. What evidence did you have? How might this lesson's emphasis on actual disagreement (rather than superficial similarity) change your approach to judging others?

General Warning Against Slanderers
Ibn Asakir warns against slanderers of the Ash'ari imams and criticizes divisive groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Asakir's stern warning against those who slander the Ash'ari imams and the danger they pose to the Muslim community.

Core Idea
Beware of slanderers who attack the Ash'ari imams — they are more harmful than Jews.

Ibn Asakir issues a passionate warning to the Muslim reader against being deceived by those who fabricate lies about the Ash'ari imams. He identifies specific groups and individuals, such as the followers of Ibn Karram and Ibn Ma? al-Harawi, and warns against relying on their biased accounts. He also condemns divisive groups from India who spread innovations and create sects, calling them more harmful than Jews. This passage is a call to vigilance and unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the warning
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the emphatic repetition of 'إياك ثم إياك' (Beware, then beware) to stress the urgency.
This double caution sets the tone for the entire passage.
2
Identify the slanderers
List the groups and individuals Ibn Asakir warns about: the Sijzis (followers of Ibn Karram), Ibn Ma? al-Harawi, and certain groups from India.
The Sijzis are known for their asceticism but false claims.
3
Understand the reasons
Ibn Asakir explains that these slanderers use lies and hypocrisy to discredit the imams and mislead the common people, often despite their outward piety.
4
Reflect on the consequences
Note the harmful effects: they divide the community, reject parts of the religion, and spread innovations. Ibn Asakir says they are worse than the Jews in harming Islam.
This is a strong statement showing the severity.
5
Apply the lesson
Consider how this warning applies today: be cautious of biased sources and those who attack scholars to create division.
The principle of 'Beware' remains relevant.

وإياك ثم إياك أيها المسلم الغيور المحتاط لدينك أن تخدع بما يختلف أمثاله في شأن الأئمة الذين جاهدوا بكل ممكن لابداء نحل هؤلاء، وهم يتبرعون بكل أفك في حقهم ليسقطوهم من أعين العامة حين ضاقت حجتهم، وأنت ترى نماذج لذلك في كتب السجزيين الإجلاف خلفاء ابن كرام السجزى وأذنابهم الذين حرمهم الله العقل والتوفيق، على تقشفهم الباعث إلى أفتتان العوام بهم إلى حين، بل في كتب الجرح ترى ما يرتكز على مختلفات هؤلاء التي يكون معها في الغالب ما ينقضها عند أهل البصيرة، ومن أبذئهم لسانا وأسوئهم اختلاقا في حق الأئمة ابن مت ذاك الهروى صاحب ذم الكلام، ثم إياك وإياك أن تعول على تراجم هؤلاء الفاتنين المفتونين فى كتب أشباههم في الزيغ وأشكالهم في الجهل المكعب حتى تعرف الدغل في معتقدهم. وبعض طوائف الهنود أصبحوا أضر على الإسلام من اليهود بنشر سخائم هؤلاء وإذاعة تلك الاهواء كان واعظ الله انسحب من صدورهم حتى ملأوا الفضاء بشرورهم لا لهم دين يزعهم ولا عقل يردعهم يسعون في تفرقة كلمة المسلمين بمذاهب يخلقونها وبدع مطمورة يحيونها حيناً بعد خين، منهم من لا يقر إلا بالقرآن ومنهم من لا يعترف إلا بالحديث ومنهم نفاة الإجماع ومنهم منكرو القياس وهلم جرا لا تنتهى وساوسهم عند حد يفترض، اختاروا أن يؤمنوا ببعض ويكفروا ببعض وأبوا أن يدخلوا في السلم كافة وسئموا أن يبقوا مسلمين حنفاء وهكذا أخذ الغريب بناصيتهم فاستاقهم حيث شاء وإذا رأيتهم يهتمون بشئ من شؤون المسلمين خارج قطرهم فاعلم أن هذا الاهتمام شوم على هذا الشأن الهام، لاساستهم ساسة ولا علماؤهم علماء يتوالى منهم على المسلمين البلاء، لا ينتظر منهم أن يرجعوا إلى رشدهم لا إذا تداركهم الله بفضله وإليه عاقبة الأمر كله.
Beware, then beware, O zealous Muslim who is cautious about his religion, lest you be deceived by those who fabricate lies about the imams who struggled with every possible means to expose the heresies of these people, and they volunteer every lie against them to drop them from the eyes of the common people when their argument became narrow. You see examples of that in the books of the Sijzis, the rude followers of Ibn Karram al-Sijzi and their tails whom Allah deprived of intellect and success, despite their asceticism that causes the common people to be seduced by them for a time. Rather, in the books of jarh you see what is based on their disagreements, which often have refutations with the people of insight. The most impudent of them in tongue and worst in fabrication against the imams is Ibn Ma? al-Harawi, author of Dhamm al-Kalam. Then beware, beware of relying on the biographies of these seducers and seduced in the books of their likes in deviance and their counterparts in compounded ignorance until you know the hidden corruption in their creed. Some groups from India have become more harmful to Islam than the Jews by spreading the hatred of these people and broadcasting those whims. It was as if the preacher of God withdrew from their hearts until they filled the space with their evils. They have neither religion to restrain them nor intellect to deter them. They strive to divide the word of the Muslims with fabricated madhabs and buried innovations that they revive time after time. Among them are those who accept only the Qur'an, those who accept only the hadith, those who reject consensus, those who reject analogy, and so on. Their obsessions do not end at a defined limit. They chose to believe in some and disbelieve in some, and refused to enter into peace completely. They became weary of remaining Hunafa' Muslims. Thus the strange one took them by the forelock and led them wherever he wished. If you see them caring about any of the affairs of Muslims outside their country, know that this care is a bad omen for this important matter. Their politicians are not politicians, and their scholars are not scholars. Calamity from them befalls Muslims. They are not expected to come to their senses, not unless Allah encompasses them with His grace. And to Him is the final outcome of all affairs.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a general warning from Ibn Asakir to the Muslim reader against deceptive slanderers who attack the Ash'ari imams. He begins with a double caution (وإياك ثم إياك) advising the zealous Muslim not to be fooled by those who fabricate lies against the imams who struggled to expose heresies. He mentions examples found in the books of the Sijzis, followers of Ibn Karram, who despite their outward asceticism spread falsehood. He points to the most impudent slanderer, Ibn Ma? al-Harawi, author of 'Dhamm al-Kalam'. He then warns against relying on the biased biographies written by these deviant groups. Next, he criticizes some groups from India who are more harmful than the Jews, as they spread division and innovation, rejecting parts of the religion and creating new sects. He lists various extremes: those who accept only Qur'an, only hadith, reject consensus, reject analogy, etc. He concludes that their leaders and scholars are incompetent, and only Allah's grace can reform them. The tone is severe, emphasizing the danger of these slanderers to the Muslim community.

Key Terms
ابن كرام
السجزية
ابن مت الهروي
ذم الكلام
الفرقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All critics of Ash'arism are impartial scholars whose opinions should be trusted.
Correction: Ibn Asakir warns that many critics, such as the Sijzis and Ibn Ma? al-Harawi, are biased slanderers who fabricate lies and should not be relied upon.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, who are more harmful to Islam than the Jews?
A) The Mu'tazila
B) Some groups from India
C) The Christians
D) The philosophers

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir's warning is a call to be vigilant against those who slander the Ash'ari imams and divide the Muslim community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear criticism of a scholar or school you admire, do you immediately defend without checking the source? How can you ensure you are not like those who accept every lie?
?
This passage warns that asceticism can deceive people. Is there a person or group whose outward piety makes you overlook their falsehoods? What will you do with that realization?
?
Ibn Asakir says some groups 'chose to believe in some and disbelieve in some.' In what subtle ways do you selectively follow Islam, picking what suits you? What is one thing you will commit to accepting fully today?

Refuting Slander with Reason and Common Sense
Ibn Asakir refutes al-Ahwazi's claim that his brother Ahmad did not pray for twenty years.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir uses logical and social reasoning to refute a defamatory claim about a scholar's neglect of prayer.

Core Idea
A claim that defies legal and social norms refutes itself.

In this passage, Ibn Asakir responds to an accusation that his brother abandoned prayer for twenty years. Rather than simply denying it, he mounts a reasoned critique: if it were true, the man would have faced execution, and the story would have spread widely. This lesson shows how to evaluate the credibility of shocking claims against known facts and social realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the accusation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the claim about Ahmad bin Ali not praying for twenty years, and note Ibn Asakir's immediate characterization of it as a lie.
The phrase 'بويلة العيد' is ambiguous but likely refers to 'the night of Eid'.
2
Identify the first proof
Notice Ibn Asakir's first argument: such a long neglect of prayer would have resulted in capital punishment. Since no such execution occurred, the claim is false.
3
Identify the second proof
Observe the second argument: if the incident had happened, it would have become widely known and transmitted. The lack of widespread report further disproves the claim.
4
Reflect on the motive
Consider Ibn Asakir's conclusion that the false accusation stems from excessive extremism (الغلو). Reflect on how ideological bias can lead to slander.
5
Summarize the method
Summarize Ibn Asakir's method: he uses legal consequences, social transmission, and rhetorical questioning to dismantle the accusation.
This approach is a model for combating baseless rumors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حكايته عن أخيه أحمد بن على الأهوازي في بويلة العيد وأنه لم يصل عشرين سنة فمن الكذب المستنكر البعيد. فيما ذكره أو حكاه، وقد تقدم في باب ذكر اجتهاده في بادة ما يكذبه وإياه، ويوضح أن أحدهما اختلف ذلك عليه وافتراه وكيف يترك إنسان الصلاة هذه المدة الطويلة فى مثل ذلك الزمان ولا يقتل، أم كيف يعرف ذاك من حال رجل ثم لا يستفيض عنه وينقل، وأى معنى في تخصيصه بويلة العيد بأنها لا تؤثر فى انتقاض الوضوء فقد ظهر أن الحامل له على التشنيع عليه بمثل هذا فرط الغلو.
As for his story about his brother Ahmad bin Ali al-Ahwazi concerning the 'night of Eid' and that he did not pray for twenty years, it is one of the outlandish and far-fetched lies. Regarding what he mentioned or narrated, and it has already preceded in the section on his diligence in worship that which refutes him? and clarifies that one of them differed on him and fabricated it. How can a person leave off prayer for this long period in such a time and not be killed? Or how can that be known about a man's condition and then not become widespread and transmitted? And what meaning is there in specifying the night of Eid as not affecting the invalidation of ablution? It became clear that what drove him to defame him with such things is excessive extremism.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ahwazi's specific claim that his brother Ahmad did not pray for twenty years. Ibn Asakir first states the claim itself, calling it an outlandish lie. He then argues that if such a thing had happened, the man would have been killed for apostasy, as leaving prayer for such a long period would have legal consequences. Moreover, such a notorious act would have become widely known and transmitted, but it was not. He also questions the specific mention of the night of Eid as not breaking wudu, which seems to be an additional absurd detail. Ibn Asakir concludes that the motivation behind this slander is excessive extremism. The passage combines logical reasoning (lack of legal punishment, lack of widespread report) to demonstrate the falsehood.

Key Terms
بويلة العيد
الوضوء
الغلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The story about Ahmad bin Ali not praying for twenty years might be true because it was reported by a credible source (his brother).
Correction: Ibn Asakir demonstrates that the claim contradicts both Islamic law (capital punishment for abandoning prayer) and social reality (it would be widely known). Thus, even if reported, it cannot be true.

Checkpoint
What logical proof does Ibn Asakir give against the claim that Ahmad did not pray for twenty years?
A) He quotes a hadith
B) He says such a person would have been killed and the news would spread
C) He denies the existence of the brother
D) He says it was a dream

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir refutes slander by appealing to legal and social norms that make the claim impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you believed or spread a story about someone without checking if it contradicts basic facts about their life and obligations? How will you use Ibn Asakir’s reasoning to be more just in the future?
?
Al-Ahwazi’s extremism drove him to slander his own brother. Is there any area of your religious practice where your zeal has led you to assume the worst about others? What step can you take to replace that with fair judgment?

Refuting the Claim about Ibn al-Sa'luqi and His Father
Ibn Asakir refutes a fabricated claim about the Sa'luqian family's stance on al-Ash'ari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir uses historical evidence and logical reasoning to refute a false claim about the Ash'ari affiliation of the Sa'luqian family.

Core Idea
The Sa'luqian family were renowned Ash'ari supporters, disproving the slander.

In this passage, Ibn Asakir confronts a fabricated story by al-Ahwazi claiming that Ibn al-Sa'luqi and his father opposed al-Ash'ari. Rather than engaging in abstract debate, Ibn Asakir presents concrete historical facts about the family's well-known role as champions of the Ash'ari madhhab in Khorasan. This refutation demonstrates the importance of biographical evidence in defending scholarly reputations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Get a feel for Ibn Asakir's confident tone and the flow of his refutation.
Notice the strong opening phrase: 'مما يقطع بأن الاهوازى كذب فيه'.
2
Identify the claim and the refutation strategy
Ibn Asakir begins by asserting that al-Ahwazi lied and misnamed the person. Then he provides evidence to prove the opposite. Observe that he does not just deny the claim; he builds a positive case.
The strategy is to overwhelm with undeniable facts.
3
Examine the historical evidence
Look at the three individuals: Abu al-Tayyib, his father Abu Sahl, and their relative Qadi Abu 'Umar. Ibn Asakir states they were the strongest supporters of both Shafi'i and Ash'ari madhhabs in Khorasan. Consider the weight of this statement.
The phrase 'أشد أهل خراسان نصرة للمذهبين' is key.
4
Understand the logical argument and conclusion
Ibn Asakir argues that if the family truly opposed al-Ash'ari, their consistent support for the Ash'ari madhhab would be hypocritical. He also references earlier praise of al-Ash'ari by Abu Sahl, concluding the falsity is clear.
Think about how fame itself serves as proof: 'لا يحتاج هذا القول إلى أن أدل عليه لشهرته'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما حكاه عن ابن الصعلوكي عن أبيه فمما يقطع بأن الاهوازى كذب فيه وأخطأ في تسميته الصعلوكي فلم يدر كيف يسميه وهو الإمام ابن الإمام الفقيه ابن الفقيه. كان أبو الطيب سهل بن محمد بن سليمان وأبوه الإمام أبو سهل الصعلوكيان وختنهما القاضي أبو عمر محمد بن الحسين أشد أهل خراسان نصرة للمذهبين مذهب الشافعى ومذهب الأشعرى فكيف خفى مثل هذا على هذا الأبله المفترى، فإن هؤلاء الثلاثة كانوا في زمانهم القائمين بالدعاء إلى مذهب الأشعرى ونصرته، ولا يحتاج هذا القول إلى أن أدل عليه لشهرته، فلو كان ما حكاه عنهما صحيحا لكان انتسابهما إلى مذهبه منهما قبيحا، وكيف يعتقد إنسان تفضيل إمام أو يقول بامامته وهو متحقق منه ما يقضي بانسلاخه من ديانته، وقد ذكرت مدح أبي سهل الصعلوكي للأشعري فيما سبق فبان كذب الاهوازي فيما تخرص واختلق.
As for what he narrated about Ibn al-Sa'luqi on the authority of his father, it is something which definitively proves that al-Ahwazi lied in it and erred in naming him al-Sa'luqi, for he did not know how to name him, while he is the imam son of an imam, the faqih son of a faqih. Abu al-Tayyib Sahl bin Muhammad bin Sulayman and his father the imam Abu Sahl al-Sa'luqiyan, and their brother-in-law the qadi Abu 'Umar Muhammad bin al-Husayn were the strongest supporters of the two madhhabs in Khorasan: the madhhab of al-Shafi'i and the madhhab of al-Ash'ari. So how could such a thing be hidden from this foolish liar? For these three were in their time the ones who called to the madhhab of al-Ash'ari and supported it. This statement does not need proof due to its fame. If what he narrated about them were true, their attribution to his madhhab would be ugly from both of them. How can a person believe in the preference of an imam or profess his imamate while being certain of him that which entails his expulsion from his religion? And I have mentioned previously the praise of Abu Sahl al-Sa'luqi for al-Ash'ari, so the lie of al-Ahwazi in what he fabricated and invented became clear.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ahwazi's claim about Ibn al-Sa'luqi and his father being opposed to al-Ash'ari. Ibn Asakir begins by asserting that this story definitively proves al-Ahwazi lied and even misnamed the person. He then provides historical evidence: Abu al-Tayyib Sahl and his father Abu Sahl al-Sa'luqiyan, along with their relative Qadi Abu 'Umar, were the strongest supporters of both the Shafi'i and Ash'ari madhhabs in Khorasan. They actively called to the Ash'ari madhhab. Such a well-known fact cannot be hidden from anyone. If the claimed opposition were true, it would be inconsistent with their known allegiance. Ibn Asakir also refers to his earlier mention of Abu Sahl's praise for al-Ash'ari, further disproving the slander.

Key Terms
ابن الصعلوكي
أبو سهل الصعلوكي
أبو الطيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Sa'luqian family was opposed to al-Ash'ari.
Correction: Ibn Asakir demonstrates that they were the strongest supporters of the Ash'ari madhhab in Khorasan, as historically recorded and widely known.

Checkpoint

B) They were the strongest supporters of Ash'arism in Khorasan

D) They were Sufis

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir refutes the slander by highlighting the Sa'luqian family's unwavering support for the Ash'ari madhhab, which was widely known and historically documented.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a negative report about a person of knowledge, do you rush to believe it or do you seek evidence like Ibn Asakir did? What harm have you caused by spreading unverified claims, and what will you do to repair it?
?
Ibn Asakir defended the reputation of an entire family based on their known track record. Is there someone in your community whose good deeds you have ignored because of a single accusation? Will you investigate and defend their honor today?
?
The liar al-Ahwazi could not even get the name right. Reflect on a time you spoke about someone's faith or character without knowing the full truth. What step will you take this week to correct that error, even if it means swallowing your pride?

Refuting the Claim of al-Ash'ari's Obscurity in Basra
Ibn Asakir refutes the claim that al-Ash'ari was not a scholar visited by others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir refutes the claim that al-Ash'ari was not visited by scholars because he was not knowledgeable, and you will see how he asserts al-Ash'ari's widespread scholarly reputation.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's knowledge was undeniable and widely recognized.

Ibn Asakir continues his detailed refutation of al-Ahwazi's fabrications. The first claim he addresses is that al-Ash'ari, while in Basra, was not sought out by scholars because he himself was not a scholar. This lesson shows how Ibn Asakir dismisses this as a product of weak faith and ignorance, and highlights the widespread acknowledgment of al-Ash'ari's scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and Ibn Asakir's response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the claim is quoted, then Ibn Asakir refutes it sharply.
2
Examine the dismissive language
Ibn Asakir uses strong words: 'weakness of religion, lack of modesty, absence of understanding.' Reflect on why such language is used in polemical refutations.
3
Analyze the rhetorical question
The passage ends with a rhetorical question: 'Does anyone deny the knowledge of Abu al-Hasan...?' This is a powerful tool to assert an undeniable truth.
4
Connect to the broader context
Consider why al-Ahwazi would make such a claim and how Ibn Asakir's response defends al-Ash'ari's scholarly authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ( أنه أقام بالبصرة لا يختلف إليه أحد من أهل العلم لأنه ليس هو من أهل العلم.. فقول حمله عليه رقة الدين وقلة الحياء وعدم الفهم، وهل ينكر علم أبي الحسن رحمه الله بشر وذكره بالعلم بين العلماء الفقهاء منتشر. وقوله أنه لم يكن له من الأصحاب إلا أربعة فقول ينكره من العلماء من سمعه بل قد صحبه جماعة أعلام كل منهم في فنه إمام، تفرقوا فى الأقطار وعلموا أهل الأمصار فكانوا للخلق هداة وإلى الحق دعاة، وعند التعليم وعاة ولما يؤدى إلى الباطل نفاة، فاستبصر بتبصيرهم الجم الغفير واهتدى بهداهم الخلق الكثير، وقد تقدم ذكر جماعة من مذكوريهم وشرح أحوالهم الفضلاء من مشهوريهم، بما فيه غنية في تكذيب الاهوازي فيما أتى به وإظهار جهله وقلة معرفته بالاشعرى وأصحابه. ومن جملة أقوال الاهوازي المختلقات الفريات قوله أن ابن عينون الضراب لم يظهر ببغداد شيأ من الكفريات. فهل في اعتقاد الاشعرى كفريات كتمها ابن عينون وأظهرها غيره من أصحابه، فتمسك بها الطاعنون ما اعتقاد ابن عينون وغيره من الأشعرية إلا أبعد اعتقاد المسائل الكفرية وهم المتمسكون بالكتاب والسنة التاركون للأسباب الجالبة.
As for his statement that 'he resided in Basra and none of the people of knowledge used to visit him because he was not one of the people of knowledge' — this is a statement that he was driven to by weakness of religion, lack of modesty, and absence of understanding. Does anyone deny the knowledge of Abu al-Hasan, may Allah have mercy on him, and his reputation for knowledge among scholars and jurists is widespread? And his statement that he had only four companions is a statement denied by those scholars who heard it. Rather, a group of prominent people accompanied him, each of them an imam in his field. They spread across the regions and taught the people of the cities. They were guides for creation, callers to truth, guardians at the time of teaching, and rejectors of what leads to falsehood. Through their guidance, the great multitude became insightful, and many people were guided by their guidance. I have already mentioned a group of them, explained their conditions, and listed their famous virtuous ones, which suffices to disprove al-Ahwazi's claims and show his ignorance and lack of knowledge about al-Ash'ari and his companions. Among the fabricated lies of al-Ahwazi is his statement that Ibn 'Uyaynah al-Dharrab did not display any blasphemies in Baghdad. Are there any blasphemies in the creed of al-Ash'ari that Ibn 'Uyaynah concealed and others among his companions revealed? The critical opponents hold onto that. The belief of Ibn 'Uyaynah and other Ash'aris is nothing but the furthest belief from blasphemous issues, and they are the ones who adhere to the Book and the Sunnah and abandon the causes that lead?
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage refutes two more of al-Ahwazi's claims. First, Ibn Asakir responds to the claim that al-Ash'ari was not visited by scholars in Basra because he was not knowledgeable. He dismisses this as stemming from weak religion and ignorance, noting that al-Ash'ari's knowledge and reputation among scholars were widespread. Second, he refutes the claim that al-Ash'ari had only four companions. Ibn Asakir states that, on the contrary, many prominent imams accompanied him, spreading across lands and teaching people. He references his earlier mention of these companions as sufficient proof. Third, he addresses the claim that Ibn 'Uyaynah al-Dharrab did not reveal any blasphemies in Baghdad, implying such blasphemies exist in Ash'arism. Ibn Asakir questions this: are there actually blasphemies in Ash'ari creed that Ibn 'Uyaynah hid and others revealed? He asserts that the belief of the Ash'aris is furthest from such blasphemous issues; they adhere to the Book and Sunnah and abandon causes that lead to disbelief (the text cuts off).

Refuting the Claim of al-Ash'ari's Few Companions
Ibn Asakir refutes the claim of few companions by listing their qualities and impact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir refutes the claim that al-Ash'ari had only four companions, and you will learn about the widespread influence of his students.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari was accompanied by many prominent imams who spread his teachings.

Al-Ahwazi claimed that al-Ash'ari had only four companions. Ibn Asakir counters this by stating that, in fact, a numerous group of prominent imams accompanied him and later spread his teachings across various lands. This lesson examines how Ibn Asakir uses the impact of these companions as evidence against the claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the companions
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the contrast between al-Ahwazi's claim and Ibn Asakir's description.
2
Identify the qualities of the companions
Ibn Asakir describes them as 'imams in their fields,' 'guides,' 'callers to truth,' 'guardians at teaching,' and 'rejectors of falsehood.' Why does he emphasize these roles?
3
Notice the reference to previous mention
Ibn Asakir says he has already mentioned these companions, which serves as a cross-reference to earlier parts of his book. This builds credibility.
4
Reflect on the purpose
The description of the companions' widespread influence undermines the claim of few followers. How does quantity and quality of students support a scholar's legacy?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ( أنه أقام بالبصرة لا يختلف إليه أحد من أهل العلم لأنه ليس هو من أهل العلم.. فقول حمله عليه رقة الدين وقلة الحياء وعدم الفهم، وهل ينكر علم أبي الحسن رحمه الله بشر وذكره بالعلم بين العلماء الفقهاء منتشر. وقوله أنه لم يكن له من الأصحاب إلا أربعة فقول ينكره من العلماء من سمعه بل قد صحبه جماعة أعلام كل منهم في فنه إمام، تفرقوا فى الأقطار وعلموا أهل الأمصار فكانوا للخلق هداة وإلى الحق دعاة، وعند التعليم وعاة ولما يؤدى إلى الباطل نفاة، فاستبصر بتبصيرهم الجم الغفير واهتدى بهداهم الخلق الكثير، وقد تقدم ذكر جماعة من مذكوريهم وشرح أحوالهم الفضلاء من مشهوريهم، بما فيه غنية في تكذيب الاهوازي فيما أتى به وإظهار جهله وقلة معرفته بالاشعرى وأصحابه. ومن جملة أقوال الاهوازي المختلقات الفريات قوله أن ابن عينون الضراب لم يظهر ببغداد شيأ من الكفريات. فهل في اعتقاد الاشعرى كفريات كتمها ابن عينون وأظهرها غيره من أصحابه، فتمسك بها الطاعنون ما اعتقاد ابن عينون وغيره من الأشعرية إلا أبعد اعتقاد المسائل الكفرية وهم المتمسكون بالكتاب والسنة التاركون للأسباب الجالبة.
As for his statement that 'he resided in Basra and none of the people of knowledge used to visit him because he was not one of the people of knowledge' — this is a statement that he was driven to by weakness of religion, lack of modesty, and absence of understanding. Does anyone deny the knowledge of Abu al-Hasan, may Allah have mercy on him, and his reputation for knowledge among scholars and jurists is widespread? And his statement that he had only four companions is a statement denied by those scholars who heard it. Rather, a group of prominent people accompanied him, each of them an imam in his field. They spread across the regions and taught the people of the cities. They were guides for creation, callers to truth, guardians at the time of teaching, and rejectors of what leads to falsehood. Through their guidance, the great multitude became insightful, and many people were guided by their guidance. I have already mentioned a group of them, explained their conditions, and listed their famous virtuous ones, which suffices to disprove al-Ahwazi's claims and show his ignorance and lack of knowledge about al-Ash'ari and his companions. Among the fabricated lies of al-Ahwazi is his statement that Ibn 'Uyaynah al-Dharrab did not display any blasphemies in Baghdad. Are there any blasphemies in the creed of al-Ash'ari that Ibn 'Uyaynah concealed and others among his companions revealed? The critical opponents hold onto that. The belief of Ibn 'Uyaynah and other Ash'aris is nothing but the furthest belief from blasphemous issues, and they are the ones who adhere to the Book and the Sunnah and abandon the causes that lead?
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage refutes two more of al-Ahwazi's claims. First, Ibn Asakir responds to the claim that al-Ash'ari was not visited by scholars in Basra because he was not knowledgeable. He dismisses this as stemming from weak religion and ignorance, noting that al-Ash'ari's knowledge and reputation among scholars were widespread. Second, he refutes the claim that al-Ash'ari had only four companions. Ibn Asakir states that, on the contrary, many prominent imams accompanied him, spreading across lands and teaching people. He references his earlier mention of these companions as sufficient proof. Third, he addresses the claim that Ibn 'Uyaynah al-Dharrab did not reveal any blasphemies in Baghdad, implying such blasphemies exist in Ash'arism. Ibn Asakir questions this: are there actually blasphemies in Ash'ari creed that Ibn 'Uyaynah hid and others revealed? He asserts that the belief of the Ash'aris is furthest from such blasphemous issues; they adhere to the Book and Sunnah and abandon causes that lead to disbelief (the text cuts off).

Refuting the Claim of Hidden Blasphemies in Ash'arism
Ibn Asakir refutes the claim that Ash'arism contains blasphemies hidden by Ibn 'Uyaynah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir refutes the claim that Ibn 'Uyaynah hid blasphemies from al-Ash'ari's creed, and you will learn that Ash'arism is far removed from disbelief.

Core Idea
Ash'ari creed contains no blasphemies; its adherents are committed to Quran and Sunnah.

The third claim Ibn Asakir refutes is that Ibn 'Uyaynah al-Dharrab did not reveal the 'blasphemies' in al-Ash'ari's teaching when he was in Baghdad, implying that such blasphemies exist. Ibn Asakir rejects this by questioning whether there are any blasphemies at all, and then affirming that Ash'arism is the farthest from disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about blasphemies

2

3
Examine the defense of Ash'arism
Ibn Asakir describes the belief of Ibn 'Uyaynah and other Ash'aris as 'the furthest belief from blasphemous issues.' He also lists their adherence to Book and Sunnah.
4
Note the incomplete ending
The passage ends abruptly with 'abandon the causes that lead...' likely to disbelief. Consider why Ibn Asakir might not complete the sentence.
The text cuts off, perhaps due to manuscript damage.

Praise of the Ash'ari School and Its Middle Path
Ibn Asakir praises the Ash'ari methodology as the balanced middle path.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir defines the Ash'ari school as a balanced middle path that combines scriptural and rational proofs while avoiding extremism of various sects.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari school is the middle path, combining revelation and reason.

Ibn Asakir, a great defender of the Ash'ari school, presents a eulogy that highlights its defining characteristics. He emphasizes that the Ash'aris remain steadfast in trials, rely on both Quran and reason, and distance themselves from the excesses of other sects. This description serves as a powerful defense of their methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the full translation to get a sense of Ibn Asakir's eulogy.
2
Identify the key method
Notice that Ibn Asakir says they combine scriptural evidences (adilla sam'iyya) with intellectual proofs (barahin 'uqul). This is the hallmark of the Ash'ari approach.
This distinguishes them from both literalists and extreme rationalists.
3
List the sects avoided
The passage mentions several sects: Mu'tazila, Mu'attila, Mujassima, Mushabbiha, Jahmiyya, Karamiyya, Salimiyya, Qadariyya, Jabriyya, Rafida, Khawarij. Note what each extreme represents.
This shows the Ash'aris as a balanced group avoiding all extremes.
4
Reflect on the claim of moderation
Ibn Asakir asserts that their school is 'the most moderate of schools' and immune to criticism. Consider why he makes this claim and how it relates to their method.
This is a rhetorical defense, but it underscores their self-perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للفتنة الصابرون على دينهم عند الاختبار والمحنة الظاهرون على عدوهم مع أطراح الانتصار والاحنة ، لا يتركون التمسك بالقرآن والحجج الأثرية ولا يسلكون فى المعقولات مسالك المعطلة القدرية، لكنهم يجمعون في مسائل الأصول بين الادلة السمعية وبراهين العقول ، ويتجنبون إفراط المعتزلة ويتنكبون طرق المعطلة ويطرحون تفريط المجسمة المشبهة ويفضحون بالبراهين عقائد الفرق المموهة ، وينكرون مذاهب الجهمية ، . وينفرون عن الكرامية والسالمية ويبطلون مقالات القدرية ، ويرذلون شبه الجبرية ويتبرؤن من الروافض والخوارج ويظهرون للواقفين عن الحق وجوه المخارج ، فمذهبهم أوسط المذاهب ومشربهم أعذب المشارب ، ومنصبهم أكرم المناصب ورتبتهم أعظم المراتب ، فلا يؤثر فيهم قدح قادح ولا يظهر فيهم جرح جارح ، وقد ذكرت فيما تقدم شرح اعتقادهم فلا يطعن فيهم إلا الذين عموا عن رشادهم .
Those who are patient upon their religion at the time of trial and affliction, who overcome their enemy while abandoning seeking victory and vengeance; they do not abandon clinging to the Quran and transmitted proofs, nor do they tread in rational matters the paths of the nullifiers and the Qadariyya; rather, they combine in matters of principles between scriptural evidences and intellectual proofs, and they avoid the excess of the Mu'tazila, and they shun the ways of the nullifiers, and they discard the negligence of the corporealists and anthropomorphists, and they expose with proofs the creeds of the disguised sects, and they deny the doctrines of the Jahmiyya, and they recoil from the Karamiyya and the Salimiyya, and they nullify the statements of the Qadariyya, and they reject the sophistries of the Jabriyya, and they disavow the Rafida and the Khawarij, and they show the ways of egress to those stopping short of the truth. So their school is the most moderate of schools, and their drinking-place is the sweetest of drinking-places, and their station is the noblest of stations, and their rank is the greatest of ranks; so no disparager's disparagement affects them, nor does any injurious injury to them appear. And I have mentioned previously the explanation of their creed, so none disparages them except those who have been blinded from their guidance.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a eulogy defining the characteristics of the Ash'ari school as a balanced middle path. Ibn Asakir begins by describing their steadfastness in trials and their reluctance to seek vengeance, highlighting their reliance on both the Quran and transmitted proofs. He then explains that they combine scriptural evidence (al-adilla al-sam'iyya) with rational proofs (barahin al-'uqul), a key Ash'ari method. He contrasts them with extremes: Mu'tazila (excessive rationalism), the nullifiers (mu'attila, who deny attributes), corporealists (mujassima) and anthropomorphists (mushabbiha), and various other sects like Jahmiyya, Karamiyya, Salimiyya, Qadariyya, Jabriyya, Rafida, and Khawarij. He concludes that their school is the most moderate and immune to criticism, and that only the blind attack them.

Key Terms
الأشعرية (al-Ash'ariyya)
الأصول (usul)
الأدلة السمعية (al-adilla al-sam'iyya)
براهين العقول (barahin al-'uqul)
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)
المعطلة (al-Mu'attila)
المجسمة (al-Mujassima)
المشبهة (al-Mushabbiha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ash'aris discard transmitted proofs for rational ones.
Correction: Ibn Asakir states they combine both; they neither abandon the Quran nor follow the paths of the nullifiers.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, what is the unique method of the Ash'aris in matters of principles (usul)?
A) They rely solely on transmitted proofs.
B) They rely solely on rational proofs.
C) They combine scriptural evidences with intellectual proofs.
D) They reject both proofs and follow personal opinion.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir presents the Ash'ari school as the balanced middle path that integrates scripture and reason, avoiding the errors of various sects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological issue, do you instinctively lean toward rational arguments or pure transmitted texts? Which tendency leads you away from the balanced middle path taught in this lesson? This week, consciously test yourself on a matter of creed: seek both a scriptural evidence and a rational proof before forming your position.
?
Ibn Asakir states that the Ash'aris are patient in trials and do not seek vengeance. Think of a recent conflict where you felt wronged. Did your response reflect this high standard? What would it have looked like to combine steadfastness with a total renunciation of retaliation? Commit to one specific change in how you handle the next personal offense.
?
The passage says only the spiritually blind disparage the Ash'ari methodology. Have you ever dismissed a mainstream Sunni school like the Ash'aris without understanding its actual teachings? What is one concrete step you can take this week to learn about it from its own sources, rather than from its critics?

Defending Ahl al-Kalam Against False Accusations
Ibn Asakir refutes a slander against kalam scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir refutes the accusation that Ahl al-Kalam are associated with philosophers and heretics, and why he considers such claims as lies and fabrications.

Core Idea
Kalam scholars expose heretics, not join them.

In this passage, Ibn Asakir vigorously defends the people of kalam against the charge that they are found in the company of philosophers, logicians, and heretics. He argues that this accusation is a baseless lie, because in reality, it is the kalam scholars who refute these groups and expose their errors. The passage also begins with a critique of the opponent's flawed understanding of knowledge itself.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Read the translation first to grasp Ibn Asakir's overall argument. Note that he is responding to a specific opponent who slandered Ahl al-Kalam.
2
Identify the two-part refutation
The passage has two parts: first, a critique of the opponent's distinction between 'ilm and other sciences; second, a defense of kalam scholars against the accusation of association with philosophers and heretics.
The second part is the main focus.
3
Analyze the rhetorical strategy
Notice how Ibn Asakir uses contrast and rhetorical questions to strengthen his case. He lists the actions of kalam scholars (refuting, warning, exposing) to show the opposite of the accusation.
This makes the refutation powerful and vivid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شعری ما معنى تفرقته بين العلم وما ذكر بعده كان القرآن والفقه والحديث غير العلم عنده وقد كان ينبغي أن يقول في العلم بالقرآن والحديث والفقه حتى يكون كلامه صحيحا قد أتى به على الوجه . ( وأما قوله ( أن أصحاب الكلام لا تجدهم إلا فى الصدر مع الفلاسفة والهندسة والمنطق والزندقة . فمن جنس ما تقدم منه من الكذب والبهتان والتمويه والمخرقة كيف يكون الأمر كما قال وهم الذين يردون عليهم ويحذرون الناس من الميل إليهم ، ويهتكون بالأدلة جميع استارهم ويظهرون ما يكتمون من أسرارهم ، ويبدون للخلق عوارهم ويبينون بعدهم من الحق ونفارهم ، وما أعجب، قول هذا الجاهل السفيهة مع الفلاسفة والهندسة كأنه لا يفرق بين وبين المنسوب إليها لغلبة الجهل عليه والوسوسة الصفة
Ash'ari? What is the meaning of his distinction between knowledge and what he mentioned after it, such that the Qur'an, fiqh, and hadith are not knowledge according to him? He should have said 'knowledge of the Qur'an, Hadith, and fiqh' so that his statement would be correct. As for his statement: 'You will not find the people of kalam except in the chest with the philosophers, geometry, logic, and heresy' — this is of the same kind as his previous lies, falsehoods, obfuscations, and fabrications. How can the matter be as he said, when they (the kalam scholars) are the ones who refute them and warn people against inclining towards them, and with proofs they tear away all their coverings, and reveal what they conceal of their secrets, and expose their flaws to creation, and show their distance from the truth and their aversion? How amazing is the statement of this ignorant foolish one: 'with the philosophers and geometry' as if he does not distinguish between [the person] and what is attributed to them, due to the dominance of ignorance and whisper upon him.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. The author begins by criticizing someone (likely al-Ahwazi) who made a distinction between 'ilm (knowledge) and Quran, fiqh, hadith, claiming they are not knowledge. Then he addresses the accusation that the people of kalam (theologians) are only found in the company of philosophers, geometry, logic, and heresy. Ibn Asakir argues that this is a lie and fabrication because the kalam scholars are actually the ones who refute these groups and warn others against them. They expose their secrets and demonstrate their distance from the truth. He expresses amazement at the ignorance of the accuser who cannot differentiate between the theologians and the heretics, attributing this confusion to the dominance of ignorance and whisperings (waswasa).

Key Terms
أصحاب الكلام
الفلاسفة
الهندسة
المنطق
الزندقة
البهتان
التمويه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Kalam scholars are influenced by philosophy and heresy because they engage with similar topics.
Correction: Ibn Asakir shows that kalam scholars are opponents of heretics, refuting them and exposing their errors.

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Asakir accuse his opponent of regarding the people of kalam?
A) They are the same as philosophers.
B) They are ignorant of hadith.
C) They are lying and fabricating about them.
D) They should be avoided.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir defends Ahl al-Kalam by showing they are opponents, not associates, of heretics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a group of Muslims being slandered, do you rush to defend them or do you silently accept the accusation? How can you emulate Ibn Asakir's courage in refuting falsehood with clear evidence?
?
Have you ever judged someone's faith or intentions based on their associations or the circles they move in? What would it take for you to correct that misjudgment, even if it means admitting you were wrong?
?
Ibn Asakir points to 'ignorance and whisperings' as the root of false accusations. What whispers do you entertain that lead you to harbor suspicions about others? Will you commit to seeking the truth before forming conclusions?
